1592402038|4.0|Louis Menand wrote a highly negative review of Lynne Truss's Eats, Shoots & Leaves for The New Yorker in June of 2004. I noticed the same problems as the reviewer, but I did not get quite as frustrated by them. Truss has no real direction; however, her argument is supported as consistently as one could hope with such a noncommittal thesis. She supplies humorous, easy-to-understand examples as she attempts to show that there is a middle ground any \\"stickler\\" for grammar can reach with their fellow sticklers, whether they be prescriptivist or descriptivist.<br /><br />Truss knows her audience. We have picked up her book to find someone to commiserate with. We may not completely understand grammar, but we love and respect it, and we hate to see it misused. Each chapter is devoted to a type of punctuation mark. Truss writes adoringly of each one, as if they are the superheroes in an alphabet world. The apostrophe is needed to keep order and understanding in the universe. The comma clears up possible misinterpretations. Colons and semi-colons are graceful and full of artistic potential. Italics, dashes and exclamation marks demand your attention. The hyphen is a humble helper. And her concluding chapter laments the decline of proper punctuation and print culture.<br /><br />Menand, on the other hand, is a proper grammarian. He spends a significant chunk of his review pointing out the technical flaws of Truss's book. A major problem the reviewer had was with the book's inconsistency. He complains: \\"Where you most expect punctuation, it may not show up at all.\\" Then again, sometimes Truss would over-punctuate. She acknowledges her own inconsistencies within the book. She explains that \\"one shouldn't be too rigid about the Oxford comma,\\" and almost tauntingly tells us we should have spotted a (grammatically unnecessary, but stylistically justified) Oxford comma somewhere in her writing. For Truss, there is an exception to almost every rule, which does not really equate to being a stickler.<br /><br />Part of the reason Eats, Shoots & Leaves is kind of a mess is because its author does not do a very good job of organizing her argument to distinguish between technical and aesthetic grammar. Menand detects this snag as well: \\"Truss has got things mixed up because she has confused two aspects of writing.\\" A solution could have been to stick to the technical aspects of proper punctuation, then included a list of exceptions at the end. Her book would have been much more successful if she had explicitly set out to prove one, the other, or both separately. However, her exceptions to rules are scattered; lack sound support tying back into the thesis; and sometimes omit any further explanation or depth other than a simple, \\"That's the way it is.\\"<br /><br />As a journalist, Menand obviously advocates more for the technical uses of punctuation. He argues that it is not the job of punctuation to add beauty or personal expression to a piece of writing. He believes that punctuation's \\"role is semantic: to add precision and complexity to meaning. It increases the information potential of strings of words.\\" I would disagree with him on this and say that Truss does a wonderful job of explaining just how much personal or artistic meaning punctuation may add to a sentence. She expertly describes the way some famous writers command their writing using their own punctuation style. Her best examples of this are in the chapter entitled \\"Airs and Graces,\\" about the semicolon and colon. When we see these marks, we can feel anticipation for what is coming next. We crave more information and we know we are about the get it. The way she describes this relatable feeling is a delight to true grammar lovers.<br /><br />Perhaps the most fascinating thing I came away with from this book is one very powerful example of the power of punctuation to shape behavior and ideology. For instance, take the comma placement in the following biblical passage: \\"Verily, I say untothee, This day thou shalt be with me in Paradise.\\" Change the punctuation around a bit and this changes into evidence of Purgatory: \\"Verily I say unto thee this day, Thou shalt be with me in Paradise.\\" This is a strong example of how punctuation influences interpretation and, in this case, changes the course of human history! How Truss found so many interesting details in the obscure history of English punctuation is truly amazing. She really seems to have done her research. Each chapter contains succinct, relevant tidbits about the history and evolution of our language's punctuation marks.<br /><br />Menand hardly acknowledges the well-researched historical aspect of Truss's book, and then turns it around into a negative: \\"In earlier times punctuation did a lot more work than it does today, and some of the work involved adjusting the timing in sentences. But this is no longer the norm, and trying to punctuate in that spirit now only makes for ambiguity and annoyance.\\" Our writer just can't win with this guy. I would turn his argument around and say that there is some beauty in punctuating intuitively. Just as a lover of biological evolution would never pass up the opportunity to play around with the Dodo bird, why should a writer pass up the chance to be in touch with a relic of their beloved language? The one complaint I have about the history in this book is that Truss goes on and on about the picture she found of the first printed semicolon, yet she does not share this picture with her readers.<br /><br />Truss advocates standing up for proper grammar whenever we see it mishandled in the streets. Yet, she mishandles it in her book. She allows concessions for the artistic expression of writers, yet not for the layman (oh, except for the greengrocer). Why is it okay for an artist to use a comma slice, but then not okay for a lowly shopkeeper to add apostrophes where none should be? Perhaps she feels you must know the rules before you are allowed to break them. That implies you must be a prescriptivist stickler before gaining permission to be a descriptivist. And so, of course,  lowly shopkeepers do not have the right even to be descriptivist.<br /><br />There are certain instances in which this book is a let-down for true grammar aficionados. In one of the few footnotes, Truss tells us that the correct comma placement for the statement \\"He shot, himself, as a child\\" is as written, as opposed to \\"He shot himself as a child.\\" I was disappointed that she did not elaborate on why one is more grammatical than the other. Frankly, glazing over this explanation left me doubting whether or not she herself understood. On the other hand, Menand seems to be quite the prescriptivist, and must resent the fact that Truss admits she is no grammarian. It is unfortunate that this knowledgeable reviewer - or someone like him -  was not consulted to fix some of this book's embarrassing punctuation mistakes.<br /><br />Menand points out that the publishers \\"elected not to make any changes for the American edition, a typesetting convenience that makes the book virtually useless for American readers.\\" I noticed this problem as well. Truss does make mention of a few differences between British and American English, which only served to peak my interest on the topic of how our two languages have and will diverge. I would have liked for her to have elaborated a bit further for her American audience. For the most part I was left feeling I would have gotten more out of her book if I were British.<br /><br />That being said, there are some things Truss chose to include in her book that were not really necessary and could have been omitted to make room for expansion of other concepts. Specifically, she goes into what I'll refer to as \\"alternative\\" punctuation. So, for instance, she suggests that a potential replacement for the apostrophe (`) could be a tilde (~). She also tells us that some advocate for the return of compound punctuation, such as commaswith dashes (, --) and semicolons with dashes (;--). Disappointingly, she does not delve any further into how these types of punctuation would improve any of today's difficulties. If anything, they would just overcomplicate a system that is naturally heading in the direction of simplification.<br /><br />Truss's treatment of the double possessive problem left me dissatisfied. I expected her to give a more definitive opinion on writing one way or the other and not allowing an exception to this rule. If the goal is to make grammar more understandable and intuitive, why not simplify rules where we find intrinsic inconsistencies and confusion? So, for instance, to say \\"a friend of Sally's\\" would be the same as saying \\"a friend of hers.\\" Yet, it is not proper to do this for inanimate objects. \\"A fan of the book's\\" or \\"a fan of its\\" are not correct. This distinction between animate and inanimate seems trivial and unnecessary.<br /><br />Despite its flaws, this grammar book had me laughing out loud, yet that is one appreciable aspect of Truss's writing that Menand did not seem to understand. The reviewer's mistake is in taking Truss too seriously. The New Yorker, understandably, means serious business. But the author is your typical quirky British lady, a la Bridget Jones. At one point Truss describes how seeing an improperly placed apostrophe incites violence and anger in her. Of course, she only means to make her audience laugh and establish some pathos with fellow sticklers. Menand seems oblivious to the idea that maybe Truss is just writing a light-hearted, accessible piece for normal, everyday grammar fans.<br /><br />Readers of this book may not come away with an instant recallable knowledge of grammar rules. It would take years of practice reading and writing with constant attention to the rules Truss presented in order for any typical reader to approach the prescriptivist heights Menand has set for himself. However, readers will come away with a greater appreciation for the language and the subtle - or not so subtle - differences its tiny punctuation marks have the power to make.

B009YU6FJM|5.0|As a fan that found the album Diamond Eyes a return-to-form after the self-titled release and the somewhat experimental Saturday Night Wrist; Koi No Yokan does not fail to please. Serving as a near-culmination of all their sounds the Deftones have  developed across all their material, this album excels in all areas, Chino Moreno's vocals have never been more spot-on or in tune with the feel of each song, Sergio Vega proves a formidable alternative to the unfortunately injured Chi Cheng on bass, choosing to flow with his own style of rhythm rather than simply \\"standing in\\" for Cheng. Abe Cunningham locks in tight in his known solid and now somewhat flashier drumming than previous titles. However, as a guitarist, I have to commend Stephen Carpenter for the seamless integration of the eight-string guitar fully into the band's sound, creating lush, deep chords juxtaposed with higher melodies, incorporating the entire instrument rather than simply hammering away on the lowest four strings in the fashion of many of the 'Djent' style bands currently releasing music these days. With Diamond Eyes now seeming like Steph's first flirtation with the eight string, in Koi No Yokan he demonstrates a mastery of the extended range and a thoughtful restraint that give the album an unprecedented depth. High points include 'Swerve City'; 'Poltergeist'; and 'Goon Sqauad', and while these tracks stand out, I am hard pressed to fin a low point anywhere on this particular record.

B002MEW7YY|3.0|When premiered, this production was important enough to warrant a review in the New York Review of the Books, by the venerable Daniel Mendelsohn issuing the most powerful vituperation towards it, especially denouncing the director for the poor taste, complete lack of understanding of the historical background, artistic insensitivity and so on.  The pounding uproar of Mendelsohn's wrath was such that it remained in memory, as not every day an opera production causes a similarly furious reaction.<br /><br />Thus I was curious to see it when by chance I came across this DVD.  First of all, I found the production rather quite conservative and lavish, very much in the Met's tradition.  There are very few liberties taken, and for those who want to see a true re-invention of the opera story, just about any recent German-speaking lands (German, Austrian, Zurich) production shall suffice.  I found this Met production quite beautiful, although not particularly striking.  In any case, it is not aesthetically offensive; it rather leaves one indifferent.<br /><br />The good things are, however, only a handful - first of all, the primadonna - La Netrebko is a fierce Lucia, a tigress as she is.  The scene of the duet with her brother shows her at her best dramatically  - fiery eyes, imposing posture.  Her voice is glorious, her vocal powers and controls are in excellent shape.  With her grandiose presence, she looks pushed into a role of a hysteriac in the mad scene, where she entangles herself with the bloody bridal veil.  Netrebko never seems to lose herself, and here in Lucia she would probably be better if she were allowed to act with premeditation, like she was in Evelino Pido's Anna Bolena.  It is perhaps due to her too strong personality of a very sane, wise and reasonable individual that she could not be a believable Lucia, or the acting was not tailored to suit her true character.  Yet her performance here is noteworthy.<br /><br />Enrico (Marius Kwiecien) is vicious enough and sings convincingly.<br /><br />Edgardo (Piotr Beczala) has an unsteady entrance and throughout the performance does not sound  terribly thrilling.  It seems that he rehearsed the best his final aria \\"Tu che a Dio spiegasti l'ali\\" which he sings with a pleasant squillo.  However in this production he is not an Edgardo who will make you feel any despair or experience any strong emotions at all.  In this quality he matches Anna Netrebko in this performance.<br /><br />The truly disappointing and even scary impressions come from the musical director Marco Armiliato.  Many times, especially in the beginning with Piotr Beczala the singer and the orchestra seem to be playing a catching-up game - either Beczala is too early or the orchestra is too late.  It happens so often and so noticeably that one wonders how can a house with inexhaustible resource make such a poor musical delivery.<br /><br />The sextet \\"Chi mi frena in tal momento?\\" (Act II, track 28) deserves a deathly thumb-down.  It is truly a musical fiasco and should be an embarrassment for the conductor.  I did not find a photographer taking away or adding anything; for that matter, no decorative trick was of any value at the moment.  The bottom-line of any opera performance is music,  and musical integrity was seriously violated in this highlight of the opera.<br /><br />One side comment for this scene, when the Edgardo bullies Lucia and condemns her publicly, is that it must have inspired Verdi for his Alfredo doing the same to Traviata, also in Act II.  But of course, that was a much better behaved French story, with a different outcome, as compared to wild Scots.<br /><br />Back to this Lucia -  it is another case of a typical Met production: expensive, elaborate, star-participating (Netrebko), but somehow the end result leaves the listener and the viewer nonchalant.  The ghosts employed to enliven the performance were amusing; the most curious was Lucia's own ghost (played by well-powedered Netrebko)who looks inquisitively at her lover and then makes the final push of the fateful dagger into his body.  This innovation must be inspired by another classic - ballet Giselle, with Lucia playing a vindictive blood-thirsting Willis, taking the life of a treacherous lover.  Yet the story is not totally convincing again - a romantic heroine, like Giselle, is supposed to save her beloved from any harm; the tradition supported by later creatures like Gilda or Senta.  Perhaps the idea was to turn Lucia into Judith, who kills unworthy lovers - first her unappealing husband, then a cruel lover.<br /><br />On the second thought, however, the idea has some merit - it should require a special skill of a woman to kill a man like she kills her husband;  anyone knows that in reality it is quite impossible for a female to kill a male with a knife without suffering any harm; this means she really killed him instantly, making a perfect hit.  The method of killing is concealed.  What did she do to Arturo?  Maybe she had castrated him, and he bled to death... I am sure such an interpretation is awaiting us soon in Berlin or Vienna, but the Met again was too shy.<br /><br />Yet this final reincarnation of Lucia as a double castrating murderess explains why an ephebe like Daniel Mendelsohn was horrified with the production. But it seems that the public cheered the idea of Lucia on the path of becoming a successful serial killer, invincible now since she's a ghost.<br /><br />Therefore this production might not be as bland and dull as initially seems.  I give it generous three stars for interesting hints and twists, in the Edgar Allen Poe style.  After all, Edgar Allen Poe could be considered a worthy heir to Sir Walter Scott.  Maybe this production should have announced itself as Ligeia de Lammermoor, although Judith gives the action more assuredness, culminating with success.who looks inquisitively at her lover and then makes the final push of the fateful dagger into his body. B005HV6Y5W|5.0|I would go out on a limb and say that there are probably not many other directors who divide the viewing public more than Terrance Malick. His films are an art form, but often they are so immersed in the art form that the viewers may lose the film before it's done. Which is a disservice. Especially since the best way to appreciate this film is by taking a step back and looking at the whole picture.<br /><br />First I must comment on the blu-ray visuals. Fantastic. This is one of those films that shines. There is even a series of scenes starting with the big bang and progressing through evolution and into the 'present'. The scene could have been very disastrous. But somehow Malick makes this work.<br /><br />Music is of utmost importance. There are many great tracks, but of note is the use of Smetana's Moldau. It fits the film perfectly. But the classical score is so well matched to each scene that I can't imagine watching this film without the score.<br /><br />If you view the events of this film as an abstract of eastern philosophy, then it makes much more sense. Malick is putting forward the belief that all life is interconnected (hence Tree of Life). The other influence is more along the Biblical sense (kind of countering the Eastern philosophy). The premise is \\"Why do we suffer?\\" and in fact this is spoken aloud in the film.<br /><br />The only two points that warrant me giving this a 98 out of 100: There is a scene where Chastain is floating around as if in a vortex - it was jarring and I felt the scene could have been better if she was skipping around in the forest maybe lighter than air? The other point was in the latter 3/4 part of the film. I think Malick drew out the scenes with the boys a little too much - I mentioned it to my partner (hey, I'm kind of wanting to move on past the boys' scenes) and then it changed right after that.<br /><br />Sean Penn isn't in the film very much, so it's a little odd to see him get second top billing along with Pitt, but I guess his name carries weight. Brad Pitt though is phenomenal and deserves much credit for his role. Even the kids are pretty fantastic. The acting is mostly subtle and there isn't a ton of dialogue, much to the style of Malick.<br /><br />I loved the film and count it among the best films in recent years. It is as close to perfection as I have seen lately. It does require one to suspend many preconceptions about film in general, but if one is able to set aside these notions and enjoy the film as a work of art as a whole, then the viewer will be rewarded.<br /><br />And I highly recommend the Blu-Ray, it looks great! B005EWDA9M|4.0|It probably makes sense to read the third volume before writing a review, but I felt the need to pen down my thoughts before I lose them in the interim. Anyway, whether out of sheer marketing considerations, or Murakami's (and his translators') conscious attempt to create a kind of cliffhanger before the third volume, the first two books are self-contained in the way they trace the trajectory of the paths of the two protagonists Aomame and Tengo, which come so close to colliding, but not just yet.<br /><br />At the heart of the story, \\"1Q84\\" stands for the alternate universe of 1984, and how it comes to being is seamlessly dealt with by the cool, clean narrative style of the author, so much so, that when the clues dropped throughout the story begin to fit the pattern of the world of 1Q84, the reader is lulled into accepting the logic of the switch - which speaks of Murakami's level of ease with the magic realism genre. Kudos must be given (again) to Jay Rubin for distilling the essence of Murakami's Japanese prose, so that the minute and methodical attention to seemingly mundane tasks like preparing a meal, or a song and its writer's background, is never seen as superfluous, but rather as essential to the world Murakami has created. Of course, there is also the attention to his characters that give a certain gravitas to them, for example, the way Aomame can disarrange her features into an alarmingly ugly scowl, and her uneven ears (and lopsided breasts - perhaps a part of the female anatomy that features prominently in the author's consciousness?), on an otherwise attractive if cold demeanor.<br /><br />Without giving the plot away too much, it is safe to peg this as a story about life imitating art, and the power of words in creating worlds which blurs fact and fiction. It is testament to the persuasiveness of Murakami's story that this reader finds himself wondering about the Little People and Air Chrysalises after putting the book down.

1590171993|4.0|Stoner is a literary and tragic novel reminiscent of Thomas Hardy if Hardy had set his novels in the American mid-west of the early 20th century. William Stoner, born on a hard scrabble family farm in Missouri changes the direction of his life after being inspired by a literature professor at the University of Missouri. As his story unfolds all the things that he holds out initially as ideals both professionally and personally seem to conspire together to disappoint him. Career rivalries, loveless marriage, estranged child, exiled lover all impact his outer word but Stoner has a stoic quality that allows him to navigate these and other difficulties and as he ages and they mount he seemingly detaches himself from external circumstances. Early in the novel as a young student he forms what may be the most meaningful relationships of his life with two fellow students and these sustain him as the novel progresses. John Williams wrote brilliantly and the language and pace of the novel subtly draws the reader into this man's life in a most compelling way. Stoner will stay with you for some time. it is a deep and very moving read.

Later, after Katrina, I revised it to a free version with a bunch of hobo recipes in it and gave the book away as Rough Living: An Urban Survival Manual. Ferris figured out how to do it - he wrote a foodie recipe book with survival skills and some cool tricks and techniques.<br /><br />But the bragging and the expensive gear? I understand the dissapoinment of those receiving defective boxes but not for the sound of the vinyls that are really very very good.    ==================================================================================    UPDATE III - Complete Review by expert site Analog Planet: Set: 9/10 , Pressing: 90+/100    ==================================================================================  I paste here the review... very interesting and helpful I would say.    A woman walks into a butcher shop. She says \\"Can I see that chicken?\\" The butcher hands it to her. She smells it in front, she smells it in back, she smells it all over and then hands it back to the butcher saying \\"Mister, this chicken stinks!\\" The butcher replies \\"Lady, could you pass a test like that?\\"    No chicken could pass that lady's test.    My point is that I felt obliged to take this Beatles box apart and examine it more closely than was probably necessary and be as critical as necessary, from my perspective, which might not be the same as yours.    Had I done all of the listening and writing first, it would have been old news by now, so I chose to go album by album. Now let's put it all in perspective in terms of concept, presentation, sound quality and value.    Clearly the concept here was not to reissue the original albums as heard in 1963-1970. Were that the case, they would sound far closer to the originals than they do. Clearly they don't as I demonstrated to myself comparing a CD made from the Sgt. Peppers... master tape with the original Parlophone pressing. They sounded remarkably similar. Not so the reissued LP.    A reissue in the strictest sense would be as close as possible a reproduction of the original. It would be done in the analog domain and every attemptwould have been made to match the original LPs' EQ. Mistakes would not be corrected, the 'de-essing' would be done using the old school real time method as it's done when necessary today by the guys who cut from analog tape.    Mistakes--all of them--would be left in, including popped \\"p\\"s etc. just as did Kevin Gray's Nat King Cole reissues for Analogue Productions.    The goal here was to improve the originals without upsetting their essence. It was more a \\"re-imagining\\" of the Beatles catalog than a \\"reissuing.\\" The best way to do that, the producers felt, was to digitize at high resolution and use that as the starting point for the \\"restoration\\". There are those who argue that EMI insisted on attenuating bass on pop and rock records as a matter of sonic policy and that bass was rolled off on Beatle albums so why not restore it on the reissues?    Clearly there's more bass, in my opinion a lot more bass, throughout this reissue set. Sometimes it sounds welcome but at other times it sounds heavy-handed. Because forty and fifty years of playing these records have worn grooves in my mind as to what they should sound like? Or because the mastering engineers went overboard?    Without hearing the tapes I'll never know. How these reissues sound to you in that regard will depend upon your system's bass performance. Overall, a majority of the EQ choices produced improvements, but sometimes not. When mono mixes were substituted for \\"reprocessed for stereo ones\\", well no argument from me!    Clearly the engineers feel that digitizing analog at high sampling and bit rates is essentially transparent to the source or they might not have done it. And once they had the music captured at 192/24 bit they also felt down-converting it to 44.1/24 wouldn't diminish the sonic quality.    Here, I definitely differ with the producers! There's a definite loss of transparency and \\"believability\\" on these albums compared to the originals. There's a definite loss of image solidity and coherence compared to the originals. You can \\"see\\" each Beatle in the original pressing's sonic picture with great clarity and precision that you simply can't on the reissue, even when the EQ makes for a much more appealing tonal balance.    Not everyone cares about this stuff, just as some will find the Rhino Doors box cut from digital masters more than adequate while others upon hearing the double 45s issued AAA will never want to go back to the box so profound are the differences and is the listening experience.    Much of this box set sounds very good on the terms set by the mastering team. However, in my opinion both Abbey Road and The Beatles are serious sonic failures, particularly the former. If you don't think so, you've not heard a UK Apple original. In the cases of those two albums, particularly Abbey Road every attempt should have been made to duplicate the originals. Abbey Road was perfect: open, transparent, soaring. Now it's heavy and thick. It plods instead of soar.    I hope in the future someone can license that tape for an AAA reissue plated and pressed at one of the top pressing plants. The Beatles original pressing is a bit hard and perhaps lacking somewhat on bottom, but the sense of being in the room and \\"seeing\\" the performers in the studio on the other side of the microphone is palpable. Not so on the reissue.    Probably were the engineers responsible for the reissues to read this they'd think I'm plain nuts. I accept. This box wasn't aimed at purist audiophiles. It was aimed at Beatles fans. Most of them will think these the best sounding Beatles albums they've ever heard, especially if Capitol's were there only reference.    Speaking of pressing quality, overall I think Rainbo did an excellent job. If I had to rate them out of 100%, they'd get a 90+% from me.    While even the best pressing in my box was not as drop dead quiet as what RTI, QRP or Pallas is capable of, Rainbo's pressings were quiet with but a few minor fleeting defects. None of the aforementioned plants are capable of perfection either. My box was bought as yours was. It wasn't a \\"special\\" reviewer sample. I paid my monies and tooks my chances.    Imagine what a job it was to press so many records, collate them, package them and box them. it must have taken the better part of a year and filled Rainbo's plant to capacity with boxes, books and jackets--while they simultaneously pressed other projects. Yet the box I opened was physically perfect in every way. They get my applause.    My phone friend Guy Hayden at EMI who oversaw the project promised to send me an Optimal box to compare but I haven't seen it. Perhaps after reading how carefully I smelled that chicken he's decided I didn't deserve it. Or perhaps he forgot so I can't at this time tell you how the two boxes compare.    As for the book. I can't believe no one's commented on the book. It's spectacular. The presentation, the paper stock, the binding, every aspect of it makes it among the, if not the best book about The Beatles ever. It may not be the most comprehensive but it surely is the smartest in terms of concept and execution.    You get an album by album \\"history\\" giving you the most salient aspects of what was happening in The Beatles' lives, set against what happened in the studio all accompanied by the most sumptuous photo spreads. Really, the book alone is worth $75-$100. It's something I'll treasure whichever versions of the albums I choose to play. My only criticism is that it's heavy. My arms got tired!    Ironically, Mr. Hayden told me a few years ago that it was the book not the record production that kept delaying the box's release. It was worth the wait because the book is a 100% success. I wish as much attention had been lavished on the pressings and especially upon the jackets! In the ideal world they would be laminated \\"fold overs\\" like the originals. What that would have done to the cost I don't know but I imagine it would have hiked the box's cost by around$100 so I understand why it wasn't done.    The questions are: why was RTI not used at least for the plating? RTI plated the first five albums but apparently only a few plated lacquers ended up being used for production. Not even RTI's Don MacInnis was aware of that. And why was RTI not used to press the records (QRP didn't have the capacity and wasn't even up and running as the job was being contemplated)? Was it cost? RTI's inability to cope with the pressing demand? We'll never know but clearly Rainbo lived up to EMI's expectations if not ours, and if you got a few bad pressings hopefully replacements were made available.    Finally there's the issue of value. Considering that a clean original UK pressing of The Beatles will set you back almost the cost of this entire box (around $300), and this box gives you the entire collection including Past Masters, which otherwise doesn't exist, this box of 180g LPs plus super book has to rate as a very, very good value. Of course I still that like The Doors AAA reissues, at some point someone will offer EMI and Apple and the surviving Beatles and the estates of John and George enough money to entice them to let the tapes have another pass while lacquers are cut directly from them. At least for starters Abbey Road should be so produced.    Yes, I smelled the chicken, but unlike that lady at the butcher's, I paid for and took home the chicken and I'm sure glad I did! Despite my clucking, it's a pretty tasty meal! P.S.: I thank everyone for their thoughtful comments along the way. I learned a lot and that's one of the best parts about having this site!  Michael Fremer -[...]

B007V65R54|5.0|We must not expect more precision than the subject matter admits.<br />Aristotle<br /><br />Not long after I embarked on the study of psychology, a vague feeling of dissatisfaction with the field began to take hold of me.  It took me a while before I realized why I felt this way, but eventually I came to understand that it was due to two factors:<br /><br />1. The limited ability of the discipline to capture the emotional truths of ordinary experience.<br /><br />2.  The high degree of uncertainty and lack of agreement about research findings.<br /><br />Perhaps I was asking too much of the discipline, too much at this time in its development.  However, in my lifetime, I really didn't see anything to indicate it was making much progress.  Quite to the contrary, all I could see was growing conflict between theoretical accounts, increasing neuro-physiologizing, and continuing contradictions between empirical investigations.<br /><br />I have come to believe that psychology will always have to be content with limitations on the generality of its findings.  They may hold for some people, some of the time, but one never can be sure on any given occasion if they apply to a particular individual in the situation at hand.<br /><br />Nate Silver in The Signal and the Noise: Why So Many Predictions Fail But Some Dont suggests I really need to change my way of thinking.  He very persuasively argues that we can never make the kind of objective predictions I am seeking, that we must learn to think probabilistically about them, and understand that there will always be a certain degree of uncertainty to any prediction.<br /><br />we must think differently about our ideasand how to test them. We must become more comfortable with probability and uncertainty. We must think more carefully about the assumptions and beliefs that we bring to a problem.<br /><br />Silver has written an important and extensively documented analysis of predictions made in wide range of subjects including the recent financial meltdown, elections (Silver correctly predicted the Obamas electoral and popular vote in the last election on his 538 Blog at the Times.), baseball player performance, weather, earthquakes, disease epidemics, the stock market, global warming, terrorist attacks, etc. The notes alone consist of 55 pages of densely annotated citations.<br /><br />He also treats at some length how we can improve on the accuracy of the predictions we make.  The more eagerly we commit to scrutinizing and testing our theories, the more readily we accept that our knowledge of the world is uncertain, the more willingly we acknowledge that perfect prediction is impossible.<br /><br />Silver confronts head on the biases that influence many predictions.  Overconfidence is an especially frequent one, as is our fundamental ignorance of probability theory.  This prevents us from thinking in terms of the conditional probability of a future event.<br /><br />The virtue in thinking probabilistically is that you will force yourself to stop and smell the dataslow down, and consider the imperfections in your thinking. Over time, you should find that this makes your decision making better.<br /><br />But nowhere does Silver treat at any length the kinds of questions that psychologists pose, say, for example, how an individual will feel about a future stressful period in their life, the likelihood that they will change a long-held habit, or the nature of their social relationships, and cognitive processes.<br /><br />Whether or not his methodology can be usefully applied to such issues is an open question.  Regardless, whatever forms his analysis would take, it would inevitably be formulated in probabilistic terms and its value would depend on a certain willingness to accept a considerable degree of uncertainty.  Thus, we circle back to the basic concerns I expressed about psychology at the outset.<br /><br />No doubt Silver will remind us once again that we will inevitably be faced with epistemological uncertainty, especially when it comes to the very considerable difficulties of predicting human behavior.

It firmly embraces all possibilities, giving validity many if not most scenarios.<br /><br />Honorable mention to Noomi Rapace as the passionate and spiritual (Shaw). A character who sought to flesh out the unanswered questions of creation her religion may have left to question. Michael Fassbender as the mission-minded (David) illustrated how only true talent can remove enough of the &#34;humanity&#34; in the character to make a believable non-living being.<br /><br />The film was beautiful in color, texture and detail. It truly encompassed how the most skilled artists and producers stretch the limits of modern technology. Filmmaking should always raise the bar of expectation in its approach to bridging reality with fantasy. The film is a visual butterfly, evolving into something more stunning to the eye as moments passed.<br /><br />As much as I liked the very concept of the film the scope of the entire endeavor seemed quite oversimplified in some ways. The thought of humans traveling that deep into space was quite grand. Not touching on very real possibilities of sickness, dementia and a host of physical and mental conditions that would have developed from the trip alone ignored reality to some extent. This is not even mentioning how much something like this would really cost.<br /><br />For Sci-Fi fans this one's a thinking person's gem. B004LSJCJM|3.0|There are some artist that forever have me banging my head against the wall, feeling that I should be down on my knees, raising a ceremonial guitar to the heavens in prayer, thanking the guitar gods that on the third day I was blessed with the sense of hearing ... instead of sitting here trying to figure out why I'm deleting half the tracks on this, the tenth outing in as many years from Joe Bonamassa.<br /><br />There are those who claim Dust Bowl is his strongest release to date, and while I can make that argument as well, that still leaves half the songs on the cutting room floor as far as I'm concerned ... though I will say that those remaining certainly are well worth your time.  This is probably one of the most cinematic albums I've ever heard from Joe, it's full of gritty visionary lyrics that counterbalance his slow driving blues, jangling guitars, and the accelerating Hammond, one that stands toe to toe with a kicking Slide, one that will remind you of what the southern States have brought to both rock n' roll and the blues.  And still I sit here, convinced that half the songs just don't work for me.<br /><br />The exceptions are \\"Dust Bowl,\\" which has been offered as a free download, and has been shaded with strat guitar tones layered to create a western soundtrack that's just crying to be used in the TV series \\"Justified,\\" and has been riding shotgun with me as I traveled through New Mexico.  \\"Tennessee Plates\\" is a roadhouse knockout sung by John Haitt, and is sure to have you downing that last shot before splintering the dance floor.  \\"Black Lung Heartache\\" is one of the best tracks on the album, a stoner's dream with lustful guitar solos that will leave you breathless. \\"Sweet Rowena\\" was penned and sung by Vince Gill who spars completely stolen signature B.B. King guitar licks with Joe, and rises to what sounds like a festive long lost Allman Brothers track.  And still, I sit here willing to dismiss the rest of the album.<br /><br />As with the afore mentioned B.B. King, Joe has finally reached that stage of his career where his personal signature moves shine through bright as the sun, and it's a true pleasure to hear him so effortlessly collaborating with other great musicians.  There's a feeling of satisfaction on Dust Bowl that comes from relentlessly touring and nonstop playing, where he finds a flawless groove and shows it off like a new penny.  His timing and phrasing are right on cue, he's no longer just an amazing guitar player, he's a musical force to be reckoned with, and in his relaxed manner, he seems very pleased to be doing just what he's doing ... and still, I sit here willing to toss out half the tracks on this great release for some reason that I may never figure out.<br /><br />Review by Jenell Keslerentioned B.B. B004L79VUI|5.0|Robbie Robertson is an odd sort of fellow, all but disappearing from the accepted music scene.  While his mates [and I use the term `mates' carefully because his fallout with his old band is the stuff of legend] were reshaping their careers on stage and in the studio, Robbie was content to work on soundtracks [Matin Scorsese], produced several artists [including Neil Diamond], has done a great deal of other music related work as a `Suite' for the industry, and released but a mere handful of solo albums in all these years, this being his fifth.  I have no idea how he became known as the voice of the band, other than the fact that one might say he was the `good looking' member, or that he was the front and center guitarist.  While he certainly was a fine guitarist, he sang but one song on the first Band album, and I don't believe that he sang at all on the second ... matter of fact, during the Last Waltz they actually turned his microphone off.<br /><br />The Band was a collective effort, everyone had talent, everyone contributed, but Robbie remembered what his old friend The Hawk had said, and that was to get those song writing credits.  I fully realize this is history, but it's important ... while the rest of The Band was more than happy to be touring, releasing an album, and generally succeeding at their craft, Robbie had an eye that was looking farther down the road, figuring that someone's name needed to be listed on the credits, and if no one else cared, he did.  And to that end, when some of his `mates' bottomed out, or could no longer carry on, he was happy to step in and purchase their share of The Band's material; rather than just reaching out as a friend and brother to lend a helping hand.  To this day, Levon is the only hold out, and has nothing to do with the man.<br /><br />\\"How To Become Clairvoyant\\" is the title of Robertson's new release, which is pretty funny when you think about it, because he's not, neither is the album, especially when the man who would like to think of himself as the voice and words of The Band, has the most success with an instrumental ... though with the likes of Eric Clapton, Steve Winwood, Trent Reznor, and Tom Morello, sitting behind the studio glass, if he couldn't come up with an least an instrumental, there's no reason on earth for him to strap on his spurs every morning.  Honestly, all Goldmine could manage to come up with was \\"A deeply personal collection of 12 tunes, bolstered by a historic collective.\\"  Rolling Stone manages to muster an off-beat tail that says nothing, perhaps because there's nothing to say, \\"When The Night Was Young, this pretty, wistful reminiscence finds the former Band man longing for the days hen he hung out with card sharks and grifters, and thought he could change the world.\\"<br /><br />William Faulkner once said that \\"the past is never dead, that in fact, it's not even past.\\"  To that, allow me to say that for Robbie Robertson, his past is truly over, all but two of his bandmates are dead, and what's going on here has nothing to do with those heady nights of blue haze and kitchen jams that stocked the fire, this is just Robertson capitalizing on a distant dream, without which, this record would be dismissed with the wave of a hand ... which is pretty much just what's happened.<br /><br />Review by Jenell Keslerwould like to think of himself as the voice and words of The Band, has the most success with an instrumental ... though with the likes of Eric Clapton, Steve Winwood, Trent Reznor, and Tom Morello, sitting behind the studio glass, if he couldn't come up with an least an instrumental, there's no reason on earth for him to strap on his spurs every morning. I have had some pretty big failures with other caramel recipes, and this recipe has worked perfectly on 3 different occasions for me.<br />If you like to bake and enjoy a few extra steps while doing it, buy this book. The only complaint I have is that the tone of this book is a little stuffy. I'm not an exact baker all the time, but the recipes have all turned out so well that I can overlook the persnickety tone.

1579129129|4.0|This hardcover is a beautiful collection of Nick Mann's photos of skulls that were prepared by a private collector, Alan Dudley. Dudley came to Winchester's attention after getting busted buying an illegal howler monkey skull. He pleaded guilty, did his service and paid his fine, and the implication is that he isn't normally one to slip up in this way. (Replicas of many species are readily available, by the way.) Dudley gets almost all of his skulls from zoos.<br /><br />Among this book's virtues are a plethora of bird, fish and reptile skulls. Photographs of mammal skulls are widely available as they are the taxonomic touchstone for mammals. The others, not so much. Partly they are less common because (I know from trying) fish and reptile skulls can be the very devil to prepare.<br /><br />Of special interest: a great assortment of hornbills, odd and fragile skulls of venomous snakes, wild pigs with their seemingly self-destructive curving tusks. Be sure to take a look at the domestic dog skulls and consider what we have done to the sturdy wolf.<br /><br />Most of the photos are by Nick Mann, who has done great work on other Workman Publishing science books as well, and most are excellent. The photos are interspersed with Winchester's wry text about skulls in art, history, human evolution, etc. These are interesting, written for a non-technical audience, but this is primarily a visual book and a beautiful addition to the natural history library.

I like how from first paragraph, the lead character was up shit's creek. You knew that from the first paragraph and onward. There were scenarios posed in this book I just wasn't sure how even a fictional character could get out of it. But Mr. Crouch not only got Ethan out of some very intense scenarios, he did so in surprisingly believable ways. It wasn't just someone trying to look for an opening so they would make sense.<br /><br />  And his descriptions of suffering...really, you are just in agony with the character. Both in this book and his other one, Run, he describes the character's suffering with such clarity that it made me glad to be sitting in my recliner in my warm home reading this as supposed to the people I was reading about.<br /><br />  I just purchased Thicker Than Blood, the complete Andrew Z. Thomas trilogy and I was hooked on the first book in the series just from the sample of it I read at the end Pines.<br /><br />  Mr. Crouch is a very phenomenal writer and I do believe he is my new favorite author. My only small beef might be with his comfort of using broken sentences. He did it frequently in Run as well and I guess that is just his writing style. Either way, I don't feel it takes away from the thrilling content of his novels, but it is a bit of a nuisance.

B007PF7MGS|4.0|For years, even before it was trendy, I was interested in the \\"paleo diet\\", which asserts that if we eat like our early ancestors did before the dawn of agriculture, we will be much healthier because our species evolved on the hunter gatherer lifestyle, not based on an agricultural lifestyle.<br /><br />In other words, homo sapiens have been surviving for 200,000 years predominantly on animals (meat, fish, fowl), fruit, nuts, and vegetables. We didn't evolve to consume milk throughout life (especially that of another species) or grains, or beans. One of the implicit arguments is that we didn't evolve to be consumers of starch, but the agricultural revolution starting around 10,000 years ago changed that, and the change has not been for the better. That is the premise of the paleo diet.<br /><br />Dr McDougall, unlike the vast majority of authors who have backed away from the stance that a low fat high carbohydrate diet is healthy for humans, sticks to his guns and provides a cogent counter-argument to the paleo premise. He claims that humans evolved on starch, and that all forms of animal tissue (protein, fat, cholesterol) degrade health. It's a bold stance in a world that has turned away from the low fat high carb diets that were popular in the 1980's.<br /><br />Dr McDougall claims that civilization was built on starch, and he's right. Without the cultivation of grains, beans, potatoes, etc., civilization as we know it would not exist. The hunter gather lifestyle cannot support the population density required to invent and build cities, roads, ocean vessels, steam engines, electricity, medicine, technology, etc. Without starch, paleo dieters wouldn't have even been born to discuss the evils of starch with each other on the internet.<br /><br />Now does he go too far with his argument that the low fat vegan diet is optimal for humans? I don't know. I don't think anyone knows for sure, but his belief stems from over 30 years of improving the health of his patients, some of who were thought to be beyond help by the medical establishment. I think he does a good job showing that, even though civilization was built on starch, once a culture gets affluent and starts adding higher and higher levels of animal products and vegetable oils, health degrades rapidly.<br /><br />I'm glad that I purchased this book, and would recommend it to open-minded health conscious friends, or anyone struggling with the diseases of western civilization (overweight, high blood pressure, heart disease, diabetes, cancer). I would also recommend it to paleo and low carb dieters who are concerned about recent research which sugests that levels of protein of over 1g/kg of body weight activates the mtor pathway, and increases cancer risk substantially. This research may also suggest that animal protein, with its higher levels of branched chain amino acids, may be worse than plant-based protein for this risk factor, but I'm not enough of an expert to discuss that (I'll just consider it and continue to try to understand the research for my own health).<br /><br />Has this book changed my eating habits? Yes. I'm experimenting with reducing meat and adding back starch. It seems that when I stop adding oils and reduce animal protein significantly, starch doesn't cause my body to store fat. I've been stuffing myself with rice, oatmeal, vegetables, and fruit, and I've lost 2 pounds in the first week (which I gained from Thanksgiving).<br /><br />Will it change my eating habits long term? I think so, but I'm taking it one day at a time for now.some of who were thought to be beyond help by the medical establishment. B0085A9HXS|4.0|Having been based in Indonesia, Evans wanted to bring Indonesian martial arts, known as pencak silat, into the world of cinema and he would do just that in 2009 working with actor and Pencak Silat expert Iko Uwais in the film \\"Merantau\\".<br /><br />The film would gain a lot of attention from audiences at South by Southwest (SXSW) in Texas and eventually win \\"Best Film Award\\" at Action Fest 2010.<br /><br />So, Evans and Uwais collaborated for an even bigger project known as \\"The Raid\\" (a.k.a. \\"The Raid: Redemption in the U.S.) in 2011.  Originally planning a large scale prison gang film, the film proved to be too complex and time consuming, but most importantly too expensive. So, a plan for creating a SWAT team taking on drugdealers became the main storyline that Evans would focus on.<br /><br />With a smaller budget, Evans worked about four months in finalizing the script, having translated from English to Indonesian and working with Iko Uwais and Yayan Ruhian in developing the choreography.  Actors that made up the SWAT team were sent to bootcamp military training with KOPASKA (the premier frogman and underwater demolition unity of the Indonesian Navy) and learned how to use the basics of weaponry, strategic attacks and defensive techniques.<br /><br />After the film was created, Sony Pictures Worldwide acquired the distribution rights and brought it Mike Shinoda of Linkin Park along with Joseph Trapanese (\\"Traitor, \\"Tron: Legacy\\" and \\"Tron: Uprising\\") to create the new musical score for  the film.<br /><br />Since the film's screening at various film festivals, \\"The Raid: Redemption\\" has received rave reviews as one of the best action films created not only in 2012 but also within the last decade.<br /><br />VIDEO:<br /><br />\\"The Raid: Redemption - Unrated Edition\\" is presented in 1080p High Definition (1:78:1 aspect ratio).  It's important to remember that the film was low budget and shot with a Panasonic AF100.  While the AF100 is a very good video camcorder optimized for HD video, one can't expect too much from this low budget film.  But with that being said, while the picture quality may not be the best for the videophile as it does have banding issues and because it is shot indoors most of the time with scenes with lighting to little lighting, it is what it is.  It's not a vibrant film but picture quality is good but not great!<br /><br />AUDIO & SUBTITLES:<br /><br />\\"The Raid: Redemption - Unrated Edition\\" is presented in Indonesian/Bahasa and English 5.1 DTS-HD MA and Portuguese, Spanish (Latin and Castilian) 5.1 Dolby Digital.  Once again, this film is low budget but on the good side, Sony did bring in Mike Shinoda to revamp the musical score (you have a choice of selecting the original or Shinoda's musical score), so the new digital music score does sound good through the front and surround channels.  The bad side is that for a film that does utilize a ton of gunfire, it would have been great to incorporate a more immersive soundscape and directional sounds with bullets whizzing from one end to the other.<br /><br />Gareth Evans is still a filmmaker playing multiple hats in the film as director and editor but I hope for his next film, he does take advantage of lossless audio and making it more immersive.  Granted, I understand this film is a low budget and because of that, the lossless soundtrack is good, but it could have been better.<br /><br />As for the acting, I prefer watching foreign language films in their own language but for those who hate reading subtitles, will be happy to know that this Blu-ray release does come with an English dub.<br /><br />Subtitles are in English, English SDH, Portuguese, Spanish (Latin and Castilian).<br /><br />SPECIAL FEATURES<br /><br />\\"The Raid: Redemption - Unrated Edition\\" comes with the following special features:<br /><br />  Audio Commentary - Featuring audio commentary by writer and director Gareth Evans.<br />  Behind the Scenes Video Blogs - (39:32) Featuring the following mini-features: Bootcamp, set location, camera and lighting, makeup and special effects, riot van, courtyard, hole drop, drug lab, Tama's office, machete gang and corridor, John vs. Mad Dog and post-production.<br />  An Evening with Gareth Evans, Mike Shinoda and Joe Trapanese - (40:40) Featuring Post-screening footage moderated by Hadrian Belove.  The three answer questions from the moderator and audience regarding the film.<br />  Behind the Music with Mike Shinoda and Joe Trapanese - (11:05) How Mike Shinoda and Joe Trapanese were selected to create the musical score and their musical approach to the music for the film.<br />  Anatomy of a Scene with Gareth Evans  - (2:15) Gareth Evans talk about the hole drop scene and where it was inspired from.<br />  A Conversation with Gareth Evans and Mike Shinoda - (11:30) A conversation between Evans and Shinoda about the hard shoot, score, stunts and themes of the film.<br />  Inside the Score - (1:23) Promo for Mike Shinoda's music involvement with \\"The Raid: Redemption\\".<br />  Claycat's the Raid - (2:56) Featuring claymation stop-motion video utilizing clay animal characters re-enacting scenes from \\"The Raid: Redemption\\".<br />  The Raid TV Show Ad (Circa 1994) - (:44) An aged anime style promo for \\"The Raid\\".<br />  Theatrical Trailer - (2:06) The theatrical trailer for \\"The Raid: Redemption\\".<br /><br />JUDGMENT CALL:<br /><br />Typically when I see soundbytes of a popcorn action film being called \\"Best Action Film of the Year\\" or \\"One of the Best Action Films in the Last Decade\\", I am often quite skeptical and try not to expect so much, to prevent myself from being disappointed.<br /><br />I've watched a lot of action films throughout my life, especially martial arts films.  So, when I first heard about \\"The Raid: Redemption\\", I approached the film with interest because it incorporated the martial art style known as Pencak Silat, shot in Indonesia by a Welsh filmmaker and featured music by Linkin Park's Mike Shinoda.  So, it's an interesting approach as I was not familiar with Gareth Evans work and was wondering why a Welsh filmmaker was in Indonesia and how Mike Shinoda took part in the film.<br /><br />But I started watching \\"The Raid: Redemption\\" and realized this film was going to be violent, possibly bloody and there is going to be a lot of fighting and weapons.  And in some way, it reminded me of older '80s Shaw Bros. films such as \\"Hong Kong Godfather\\", where the small amount of people who represent the good, take on a large force which consists of the bad.  And for those who have watched enough martial arts films, there is a banality to these martial arts films but its all about execution.<br /><br />\\"The Raid: Redemption - Unrated Edition\\" is an exciting non-stop action film that just keeps coming at you.  Action scene after action scene, you're just captivated by this fatalistic suicide mission.  It's one thing to have 20 elite SWAT team members, but it's another thing to go against dozens of gangsters, drug users that live in the 15+ story apartment building.  You know the body count will be high but you are pulling for the rookie, Rama.  Rama represents the clean cut family man who happens to be a rookie of the SWAT team but also the more reserved member who can kick ass!<br /><br />The fight scenes showcasing pencak silat is amazing!  What I enjoyed about the approach of fighting choreography for this film is to not having these fight scenes perfectly choreographed.  Mistakes happen, there is no clear edge of who will win and as Iko Uwais who plays the main protagonist Rama was pretty cool in execution, actor Yayan Ruhian who plays the evil \\"Mad Dog\\" is amazing.  At first, I thought this was your typical skinny guy with a gun, but instead this guy was showcasing silat movement that is so slick and smooth, you start to realize that this is the guy who will be doing the butt kicking and are not sure any of the good guys including Rama have a chance againsthim.<br /><br />But the violent action scenes are intriguing at times, where one stab is not enough, some disable or kill their enemy, may it be an axe to the neck or repeated stabs to the heart, the fight scenes are violent but it fit the moment where these SWAT team people have to use what they can to survive.<br /><br />But there is an opposite to all this that made me scratch my head at times.  Where we see other SWAT team members using their knives or whatever they have to fight, Rama was one who chose to use his fists instead of the weapons that lie around the ground.  There were times where I was asking myself, is this guy ever going to pick up a weapon on the floor to fight back?  These guys are coming at you with machetes and there are police batons, machetes, guns laying around, is he going to fight back.<br /><br />Also, when the SWAT team are fighting, you would think that it would be beneficial to them to take the ammunition from fallen SWAT team officers, just in case.  But I had to remind myself that this is a popcorn action film, and not to think so seriously about character motivation or logic.  It's a popcorn action film meant to to be enjoyed for its action, not a film for one to  think too deeply about situations.<br /><br />As for the Blu-ray release, there are over a 100 minutes of special features but I noticed that many of them involved Mike Shinoda, which was cool but it would have been cooler to have the Indonesian talent featured as well.  It would have been great to feature more interviews with Iko Iwais, Joe Taslim, Donny Alamsyah, Yayan Ruhian, Pierre Gruno, Ray Sahetapy and more.  As for the picture and audio quality, while I'm sure on DVD, this film would look and sound very good... on Blu-ray, it's not the most vibrant film and you do experience some banding issues.  And you tend to wish that there was much better sound editing to make this film much more immersive for the audiophiles who expect a lot from action-intense films.<br /><br />But when it comes to a martial arts film with non-stop action, I will admit that \\"The Raid: Redemption\\" is a pulse-pounding, exciting action film and I have to agree... it is one of the better action film that I have seen within the last decade and yes, this year.  Gareth Evans and crew were able to utilize what they had, with what small budget and equipment they had at the time and they succeeded in creating such a fantastic, kick ass, all-action film!<br /><br />Overall, if you love action films, \\"The Raid: Redemption - Unrated Edition\\" is a must-see!ke Shinoda.  So, it's an interesting approach as I was not familiar with Gareth Evans work and was wondering why a Welsh filmmaker was in Indonesia and how Mike Shinoda took part in the film.<br /><br />But I started watching \\"The Raid: Redemption\\" and realized this film was going to be violent, possibly bloody and there is going to be a lot of fighting and weapons.  And in some way, it reminded me of older '80s Shaw Bros. films such as \\"Hong Kong Godfather\\", where the small amount of people who represent the good, take on a large force which consists of the bad.  And for those who have watched enough martial arts films, there is a banality to these martial arts films but its all about execution.<br /><br />\\"The Raid: Redemption - Unrated Edition\\" is an exciting non-stop action film that just keeps coming at you.  Action scene after action scene, you're just captivated by this fatalistic suicide mission.  It's one thing to have 20 elite SWAT team members, but it's another thing to go against dozens of gangsters, drug users that live in the 15+ story apartment building.  You know the body count will be high but you are pulling for the rookie, Rama.  Rama represents the clean cut family man who happens to be a rookie of the SWAT team but also the more reserved member who can kick ass!<br /><br />The fight scenes showcasing pencak silat is amazing!  What I enjoyed about the approach of fighting choreography for this film is to not having these fight scenes perfectly choreographed.  Mistakes happen, there is no clear edge of who will win and as Iko Uwais who plays the main protagonist Rama was pretty cool in execution, actor Yayan Ruhian who plays the evil \\"Mad Dog\\" is amazing.  At first, I thought this was your typical skinny guy with a gun, but instead this guy was showcasing silat movement that is so slick and smooth, you start to realize that this is the guy who will be doing the butt kicking and are not sure any of the good guys including Rama have a chance againsthim.<br /><br />But the violent action scenes are intriguing at times, where one stab is not enough, some disable or kill their enemy, may it be an axe to the neck or repeated stabs to the heart, the fight scenes are violent but it fit the moment where these SWAT team people have to use what they can to survive.<br /><br />But there is an opposite to all this that made me scratch my head at times.  Where we see other SWAT team members using their knives or whatever they have to fight, Rama was one who chose to use his fists instead of the weapons that lie around the ground.  There were times where I was asking myself, is this guy ever going to pick up a weapon on the floor to fight back?  These guys are coming at you with machetes and there are police batons, machetes, guns laying around, is he going to fight back.<br /><br />Also, when the SWAT team are fighting, you would think that it would be beneficial to them to take the ammunition from fallen SWAT team officers, just in case.  But I had to remind myself that this is a popcorn action film, and not to think so seriously about character motivation or logic.  It's a popcorn action film meant to to be enjoyed for its action, not a film for one to  think too deeply about situations.<br /><br />As for the Blu-ray release, there are over a 100 minutes of special features but I noticed that many of them involved Mike Shinoda, which was cool but it would have been cooler to have the Indonesian talent featured as well.  It would have been great to feature more interviews with Iko Iwais, Joe Taslim, Donny Alamsyah, Yayan Ruhian, Pierre Gruno, Ray Sahetapy and more.  As for the picture and audio quality, while I'm sure on DVD, this film would look and sound very good... on Blu-ray, it's not the most vibrant film and you do experience some banding issues.  And you tend to wish that there was much better sound editing to make this film much more immersive for the audiophiles who expect a lot from action-intense films.<br /><br />But when it comes to a martial arts film with non-stop action, I will admit that \\"The Raid: Redemption\\" is a pulse-pounding, exciting action film and I have to agree... it is one of the better action film that I have seen within the last decade and yes, this year.  Gareth Evans and crew were able to utilize what they had, with what small budget and equipment they had at the time and they succeeded in creating such a fantastic, kick ass, all-action film!<br /><br />Overall, if you love action films, \\"The Raid: Redemption - Unrated Edition\\" is a must-see!

B007K8ILUQ|4.0|Stanley Kubrick, one of America's legendary filmmakers responsible for films such as \\"Paths of Glory\\" (1957), \\"Spartacus\\" (1960), \\"Dr. Strangelove or: How I Learned to Stop Worrying and Love the Bomb\\" (1964), \\"2001: A Space Odyssey\\" (1968), \\"A Clockwork Orange\\" (1971), \\"The Shining\\" (1980), \\"Full Metal Jacket\\" (1987), to name a few.<br /><br />But all filmmakers have their beginnings and for Stanley Kubrick, his first feature film would be the 1953 film \\"Fear and Desire\\".<br /><br />Having created two documentaries (\\"Day of the Fight\\", \\"Flying Padre: An RKO-Pathe Screenliner\\") in 1951 and employed at \\"LOOK\\" Magazine, Kubrick quite his job to create his first feature film.<br /><br />Using funds that were raised by his family and friends, Kubrick and a classmate from his old high school, Howard Sackler, would go on to create \\"Fear and Desire\\".<br /><br />While not a box office hit, the film captured the attention of film critics, who took notice of Kubrick's talent.<br /><br />But among the Kubrick films available, \\"Fear and Desire\\" has only been screened at very few places since it's release.  One of those screenings were at the Library of Congress in Washington, D.C. back in 1993.  The film also has not been released commercially and even was downplayed by Kubrick, when he was alive, calling the film a \\"bumbling amateur film exercise\\".<br /><br />But in 2010, Turner Classic Movies aired a restored print courtesy of the George Eastman House and now, Kino Lorber will be releasing the film on Blu-ray and DVD in October 2012.  The version of \\"Fear and Desire\\" presented was mastered in HD from archival 35mm elements newly restored by the Library of Congress.<br /><br />The Blu-ray and DVD release will also include \\"The Seafarers, a short film created in 1953, a film which Kubrick took on in behalf of the Seafarers International Union in order to raise money for his next film \\"Killer's Kiss\\" (1954).<br /><br />\\"Fear and Desire\\" revolves around four soldiers who have crash landed in enemy territory.<br /><br />Lt. Corby (portrayed by Kenneth Harp), Sgt. Mac (portrayed by Frank Silvera), Pvt. Fletcher (portrayed by Stephen Coit)  and Pvt. Sidney (portrayed by Paul Mazursky) are six miles away from where they should be and know they must cross a river in order to survive.<br /><br />So, the four led by Lt. Corby, must evade enemy forces and create a raft and during nighttime, try to escape.<br /><br />But while building the raft, they are afraid they may have been spotted by a plane flying above them, so the four leave the raft and try to see what is out there in the surrounding area.<br /><br />The group spots a cabin where two enemy soldiers are eating.  Seeing their weapons and food, the four knows they must take the soldiers out and so, they devise a plan and are able to kill the two soldiers including one enemy soldier returning to the cabin.<br /><br />But Pvt. Sidney begins to crack after he sees the dead mean staring at him.<br /><br />As the four begin to work on their raft and see what is ahead of them near the river, a woman is seen catching fish with other women in the river.  While going home, she hears a noise (made by the soldiers hiding behind a bush).  The soldiers take her, bind her and try to interrogate her, but she does not speak any English.  So, the three continue their reconnaissance and leave the unstable Pvt. Sidney to watch over her.<br /><br />But with Pvt. Sidney's mind becoming more unstable, can the soldiers trust him to watch the woman?<br /><br />VIDEO:<br /><br />\\"Fear and Desire\\" is presented in 1080p High Definition (1:33:1, black and white) and features the rare George Eastman House print that is mastered in HD from archival 35 mm elements newly restored by the Library of Congress.  For the most part, picture quality for this film is very good considering its age.  I detected no film warping, excessive blurring or a lot of scratches.  There are nicks and dust that can be seen but by no means, doesit prevent you from enjoying the film.  Whites and grays were well-contrast, black levels were inky and dark.  Overall, picture quality of \\"Fear and Desire\\" on Blu-ray is not pristine but the film looks great!<br /><br />As for \\"The Seafarers\\", the film is presented in color but definitely looks its age.  While in HD, it does have that feeling of early '50s documentary shorts.<br /><br />AUDIO & SUBTITLES:<br /><br />\\"Fear and Desire\\" is presented in monaural LPCM 2.0.  I detected no major hissing or pops during my viewing of \\"Fear and Desire\\".  Audio was clear and dialogue can be heard clearly with no problems whatsoever.  It's important to remember that this low-budget film was shot without sound and dialogue and effects were added during post production.<br /><br />There are no subtitles included on this Blu-ray release.<br /><br />SPECIAL FEATURES<br /><br />\\"Fear and Desire\\" comes with Kubrick's 1953 short \\"The Seafarers\\" which was preserved by the Museum of Modern Art.  The Seafarers is a industrial documentary promoting the Seafarers International Union.<br /><br />JUDGMENT CALL:<br /><br />\\"Fear and Desire\\", the first film by Stanley Kubrick that he never wanted people to see.<br /><br />Who can blame Kubrick?  Having a remarkable list of films in his oeuvre, his first film was seen by him as amateurish and low-budget.  And as a filmmaker, one can easily criticize their earliest work and would rather have people remember the popular films that he's known for, not his first film that he created at 24.<br /><br />But for cinema fans, \\"Fear and Desire\\" is fascinating American cinema for the fact that it shows that Stanley Kubrick was a filmmaker and writer that was ahead of his time.<br /><br />\\"Fear and Desire\\" is cleverly written to poke at America's continuing involvement in war.  And while \\"Fear and Desire\\" is not about the United States but people of an unknown country, Kubrick tries to relate one's feeling towards war, the futility of the actions of government and like its title, our soldiers that are sent to fight in a war instead of live for their families, are in fear of their lives being taken and their desire to survive another day.<br /><br />For Kubrick's first film, the use of cinematography and clever editing worked to the film's efficacy.  From the facial expressions and the eyes of the soldiers, the sight of a dead person as he grasps the food to show signs of life until no movement can be seen.  Also, closeups showcasing the sight of death or insanity for a character to wide angle shots showcasing the river and the wilderness.  Kubrick's cinematography was amazing to see at his young age and once again, showing how he was ahead of his time.<br /><br />Also, intriguing was the use of two characters playing the enemy.  Actors Kenneth Harp who plays Lt. Corby also plays the enemy general and Stephen Coit plays Pvt. Fletcher and the enemy captain.<br /><br />Kubrick utilizes each character effectively.  From the young woman (portrayed by Virginia Leith) with fear on her face as the unstable Pvt. Sidney starts to go insane and rubs his face all over her.  While a seen between Kenneth Harp's two characters who encounter each other is quite a memorable sight.<br /><br />For the most part, Stanely Kubrick was able to craft an intelligent film that pokes on society during that era on war.  Something he focuses a lot decades later in his film \\"Full Metal Jacket\\".<br /><br />As for the Blu-ray release of \\"Fear and Desire\\", the fact that many people were exposed to bootlegs, because the film was never released should be happy that the 35 mm elements was restored and that Kubrick fans finally get their wish for an official video release.
